  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_b5a67372-1c78-489d-87a9-e6738c811930'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<div id="review_157109470" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/157109470" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/520753-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402541317p2/520753.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/157109470?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/520753-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/520753-kim?shelf=holy-shit">holy-shit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/520753-kim?shelf=maurice">maurice</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/520753-kim?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/520753-kim?shelf=the-kids-are-all-right">the-kids-are-all-right</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer157109470" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1486714160133052453"><i>I don’t want to<br/><br/>Because boys<br/><br/>Don’t write poetry.<br/><br/>Girls do.</i><br/><br/><br/><br/>First page, first poem. <br/>Makes me smile <br/>But also makes me kinda<br/>Sad.<br/>Do words in poem form <br/>Make you sad? <br/>I hope not <br/>but I <br/>Understand,<br/> if it does. <br/><br/><i>Love That Dog </i>takes less than 3 minutes to read. Okay, maybe a bit more if you’re on your 4th glass of Sangria <i>(but who’s counting)</i> and you linger on phrases. Phrases like:<br/><br/><i> ‘and jumping on me<br/> his shaggy straggly paws<br/> on my chest <br/>like he was trying <br/>to hug the inside <br/>right out of me’</i><br/><br/>Poems, PO-<i>EMS. </i> They</span>
  <span id="freeText1486714160133052453" style="display:none"><i>I don’t want to<br /><br />Because boys<br /><br />Don’t write poetry.<br /><br />Girls do.</i><br /><br /><br /><br />First page, first poem. <br />Makes me smile <br />But also makes me kinda<br />Sad.<br />Do words in poem form <br />Make you sad? <br />I hope not <br />but I <br />Understand,<br /> if it does. <br /><br /><i>Love That Dog </i>takes less than 3 minutes to read. Okay, maybe a bit more if you’re on your 4th glass of Sangria <i>(but who’s counting)</i> and you linger on phrases. Phrases like:<br /><br /><i> ‘and jumping on me<br /> his shaggy straggly paws<br /> on my chest <br />like he was trying <br />to hug the inside <br />right out of me’</i><br /><br />Poems, PO-<i>EMS. </i> They are not so easy to write. They look easy but try putting a whole bunch of words in short sentences and make them make someone else feel something. Go on. But, remember that people will REALLY judge you because poetry can be pretentious and there’s extra pressure on you to be all like <i>‘she walks in beauty like the night’</i> or <i>‘I want a love like me thinking of you thinking of me thinking of you type love..’ </i><br /><br />I am scared. <br />I couldn’t do it<br />Even this attempt <br />Is lame.<br /><br />But, I was lucky to know a poet, and was lucky to read his words. His voice had the right cadence, the exact urgency , the strength to leave you breathless and make you ache. <br /><br /><i>All alone in triplets I think about her laugh, even if I’m sad<br />All alone I justify our secret world and could tell the nay sayers to<br />Leave her alone i am no monster<br /><br />In prayers i disbelieve i asked for you to come<br /><br />Maybe my or god’s will<br /><br />Maybe a cessation of thinking when it comes to the enemy when i hide <br />In my foxhole would be a good idea<br /></i><br /><br />Jack is lucky to have a Miss Stretchberry. Everyone should have their very own. One that can give them worlds created by Frost and Blake and also William Carlos Williams among other amazing poets. Thank you, Sharon Creech, for giving us Jack. I used to always ask my poet ‘but what does it mean?’ and he used to say ‘it doesn’t really matter does it? How do you feel?’ <br /><br />It takes an fierce will and a tremendous heart to be a poet. <br />This book has both. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1486714160133052453" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

It's really heartbreaking when his dog died and Jack no longer wants to have another pet because of the trauma he got from losing one. The short and bittersweet poem he wrote, in the end, is like a memorial for his dog.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1544400486" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2404942532915777852"><b>Before reading, take a quick look at the short poems at the back of this book because the story will make more sense if you do.</b> <br/><br/>I saw this book at my local library and couldn't resist the bright cover, the fact that it won multiple awards, <b>was short-listed for the 2001 Carnegie medal</b> and that the Guardian called it quirky, original and 'defies categorisations' sealed the deal. I later found out (after looking it up on GR) that Ms Creech also wrote <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53496.Walk_Two_Moons' title='Walk Two Moons by Sharon Creech' rel='nofollow noopener'>Walk Two Moons</a>, my favourite book growing up whi</span>
  <span id="freeText2404942532915777852" style="display:none"><b>Before reading, take a quick look at the short poems at the back of this book because the story will make more sense if you do.</b> <br><br>I saw this book at my local library and couldn't resist the bright cover, the fact that it won multiple awards, <b>was short-listed for the 2001 Carnegie medal</b> and that the Guardian called it quirky, original and 'defies categorisations' sealed the deal. I later found out (after looking it up on GR) that Ms Creech also wrote <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53496.Walk_Two_Moons" title="Walk Two Moons by Sharon Creech" rel="nofollow noopener">Walk Two Moons</a>, my favourite book growing up which also won multiple awards.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1455391526i/18097270.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>This is an adorable story with a hint of tragedy about a boy who slowly discovers that poetry is not just for girls. He soon learns to tell his own story in the form of poetic diary entries and to write about the tragic death of his beloved dog, Sky. I also enjoyed his comment on the works of other poets e.g. Robert Frost, William Blake and others and also admire a certain poet. <br><br><b> This book is great for reluctant young readers.</b><br>initially, I got <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53498.Love_That_Dog__Jack___1_" title="Love That Dog (Jack, #1) by Sharon Creech" rel="nofollow noopener">Love That Dog</a> for my kids but it's one of those books that grow on you and before long, we were all reading and enjoying the little boy's humour and his growing confidence in copying other poets work and writing his own poetry.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1455388189i/18096719.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>The Red Wheelbarrow by William Carlos Williams</b><br><br>so much depends<br>upon<br><br>a red wheel<br>barrow<br><br>glazed with rain<br>water<br><br>beside the white<br>chickens.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1455387945i/18096619._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>The little boy's attempt...</b><br><br>"Do you promise<br> not to read it<br> out loud?<br> Do you promise<br> not to put it <br> on the board?<br><br>Okay, here it is,<br>but I don't like it<br> <br><i>So much depends<br>upon<br>a blue care<br>splattered with mud<br>speeding down the road.</i><br><br>What do you mean-<br><i>Why does so much depend<br>upon<br>a blue car?</i><br><br>You didn't say before<br>that I had to tell <i>why.</i><br>The Wheelbarrow guy<br>didn't tell <i>why.</i><br><br>I'm glad to have looked at the back of the book and read the short poems being referred to by the little boy, otherwise the humour and innocent jab at other poets, would've gone over my head. </span>

  <span id="freeText15092348811962065168" style="display:none">A very very short book full of simple poems written from a 7 y/o's POV. I came across this book one day when I was really bored and had nothing to read. For me, a few poems still lingered on me after finishing it.<br /><br />Jack, the MC, told a story of his relationship with his dog in this book, which was really sweet. He attempted to share his story through a series of poems. <div id="review_1336020935" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1336020935" />
    <a title="Margaret" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/991805-margaret"><img alt="Margaret" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1207023483p2/991805.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1336020935?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margaret" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margaret" href="/user/show/991805-margaret">Margaret</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">parents, kids, teachers</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Margaret by: </span>
      Jackie, a great fourth grade teacher
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1336020935" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15875500365360523582"> This is a second read-through of an entertaining and educational little book for children about writing poetry. It’s set in an elementary school classroom and begins with Jack writing a complaining note to his teacher that “boys / don’t write poetry. / / Girls do.” But, of course, it is no surprise that this boy will write poetry and at least some of it will be about a much-loved dog.<br/><br/>	Apart from the relatively thin storyline, this book makes its point about the power of poetry (and of persisten</span>
  <span id="freeText15875500365360523582" style="display:none"> This is a second read-through of an entertaining and educational little book for children about writing poetry. It’s set in an elementary school classroom and begins with Jack writing a complaining note to his teacher that “boys / don’t write poetry. / / Girls do.” But, of course, it is no surprise that this boy will write poetry and at least some of it will be about a much-loved dog.<br /><br />	Apart from the relatively thin storyline, this book makes its point about the power of poetry (and of persistent teachers) by including Jack’s responses to poems the teacher shares with her students. (All eight poems are also included at the end of the book, which means that in addition to the story, the children get to read those eight poems.) And so we get to watch Jack figure out that “the wheelbarrow poet [William Carlos Williams] / was just / making a picture / with words.” Maybe, Jack suggests, Robert Frost was doing the same thing in his snowy woods poem. In addition to figuring out what imagery might be, Jack also learns to listen to the rhythms of poetry as he makes up a poem about a blue car while “some of the tiger sounds [of Blake’s “The Tiger”] / are still in my ears / like drums / beat-beat-beating.” From Arnold Adoff’s “Street Music” Jack learns to use spacing and fonts to create effects; from S. C. Rigg’s “The Apple,” he learns to recognize and create his own shaped poem. In addition, the readers get an appearance by Walter Dean Meyers, who comes to visit the classroom in response to Jack’s written invitation. It was Meyers' poem “Love That Boy” that first convinced Jack that poetry was for him. And Jack finally does come to terms with the death of his dog Sky by writing a poem. And after Meyers’ visit, Jack writes another poem about Sky called "Love That Dog" <i>inspired</i> by, you guessed it, “Love That Boy.” <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15875500365360523582" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/978959494?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer978959494" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8079046346888964746">Love that Book!<br/><br/>What a beautiful way to show how a child may slowly start to appreciate poetry by trying it out himself. The development is subtle, starting with aversion (I don't want to because boys don't write poetry. Girls do.), then copying of form, rhythm, meaningful lines from famous poets, finally first own authorship and proud signing of a poem: BY JACK!<br/>I love the fact that Jack's first poem has a secret meaning we can't grasp until he has told the story of his beloved dog at the end of </span>
  <span id="freeText8079046346888964746" style="display:none">Love that Book!<br /><br />What a beautiful way to show how a child may slowly start to appreciate poetry by trying it out himself. The development is subtle, starting with aversion (I don't want to because boys don't write poetry. Girls do.), then copying of form, rhythm, meaningful lines from famous poets, finally first own authorship and proud signing of a poem: BY JACK!<br />I love the fact that Jack's first poem has a secret meaning we can't grasp until he has told the story of his beloved dog at the end of the book. <br />It is amazing to be part of his experience of William Blake's beat in Tyger, Tyger, how he transforms the wild, fearful tiger of nature into a blue, speeding car, modern and urban but equally dangerous and unpredictable.<br /><br />Read<br />This<br />Book!</span>

<div id="review_74392213" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74392213" />
    <a title="Missy Kutyla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2765024-missy-kutyla"><img alt="Missy Kutyla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1253646208p2/2765024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/74392213?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Missy Kutyla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Missy Kutyla" href="/user/show/2765024-missy-kutyla">Missy Kutyla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060292874" href="/book/show/1592075.Love_That_Dog">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2765024-missy-kutyla?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer74392213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16507285346952365454">I am so happy Dr. Junko Yokota suggested this novel to read because it is one of her favorites. I feel Sharon Creech , winner of the Newbery Medal for Walk Two Moons, took me to a place that was full of sounds, images, and forms. This short novel is a weekly journal entry from the point of view of Jack, a young student, initially appalled at the suggestions to read, let alone, write a poem. Each week a poem was recited to the class and his journaling about the assignment showed a progression of </span>
  <span id="freeText16507285346952365454" style="display:none">I am so happy Dr. Junko Yokota suggested this novel to read because it is one of her favorites. I feel Sharon Creech , winner of the Newbery Medal for Walk Two Moons, took me to a place that was full of sounds, images, and forms. This short novel is a weekly journal entry from the point of view of Jack, a young student, initially appalled at the suggestions to read, let alone, write a poem. Each week a poem was recited to the class and his journaling about the assignment showed a progression of his emotional struggles and successes of putting "himself" out there with poetry. Jack found his favorite poet and grows into his "own" to help others write their own poetry. The rhythm steadily gains speed and you will not want to put the book down until the very end. I read it aloud and had to stop using my voice while my eyes kept up with the pace. I found my throat swelled and even a tear touched my cheek and then off to another celebration of experiences that I was connecting to. A pause I was a bit embarrassed to explain to the hubby about. I feel Sharon Creech demonstrates how Jack evolved by having access to wonderfully diverse and rich poetry and embodied this lesson in a cute verse with more space between the lines like how Jack wrote it the first time. This is a great read for fourth through sixth grades and feel everyone can be drawn in by Jack's poetic process.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16507285346952365454" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_19354416" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19354416" />
    <a title="Joanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/999637-joanna"><img alt="Joanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1210888162p2/999637.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19354416?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joanna" href="/user/show/999637-joanna">Joanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/999637-joanna?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/999637-joanna?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19354416" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13619165051636283811">I love love love this book! It was my favorite by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11633.Sharon_Creech' title='Sharon Creech' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sharon Creech</a> until she published <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53492.Heartbeat' title='Heartbeat by Sharon Creech' rel='nofollow noopener'>Heartbeat</a>
</i>, and now I can't say which I like more. It is written entirely in free verse written in the school notebook of a boy who is a reluctant poetry pupil. The poetic form, believability of Jack (the main character), and the story he ultimately tells are simply smashing. This book would also be a great way to introduce young people to poetry; Jack's responses to a few well-known poems are written in his n</span>
  <span id="freeText13619165051636283811" style="display:none">I love love love this book! It was my favorite by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11633.Sharon_Creech" title="Sharon Creech" rel="nofollow noopener">Sharon Creech</a> until she published <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53492.Heartbeat" title="Heartbeat by Sharon Creech" rel="nofollow noopener">Heartbeat</a>
</i>, and now I can't say which I like more. It is written entirely in free verse written in the school notebook of a boy who is a reluctant poetry pupil. The poetic form, believability of Jack (the main character), and the story he ultimately tells are simply smashing. This book would also be a great way to introduce young people to poetry; Jack's responses to a few well-known poems are written in his notebook, and the text of the poems is included in the back of the book (<a href="http://www.poets.org/viewmedia.php/prmMID/15537" rel="nofollow noopener">The Red Wheelbarrow</a> by William Carlos Williams, <a href="http://www.sparknotes.com/poetry/frost/section10.rhtml" rel="nofollow noopener">Stopping by Woods on a Snowy Evening</a> and <a href="http://www.bartleby.com/118/1.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The Pasture</a> by Robert Frost, part of <a href="http://www.bartleby.com/101/489.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The Tiger</a> by William Blake, dog by Valerie Worth, Street Music by Arnold Adoff, The Apple by S.C. Rigg, and Love That Boy by Walter Dean Myers). But you really have to read an <a href="http://www.sharoncreech.com/excerpt/01.asp" rel="nofollow noopener">excerpt</a> of the book to see how neat it is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13619165051636283811" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer819362729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11116621899641018939"><b>Review #3 - January 10, 2018</b><br/><br/>I love poetry and I love this book. I read it to my students every year and I never get tired of it. (It also makes me cry every freaking time.) <br/><br/>In my class, we look at one or two famous poems each week. This year, before I read the book, I made sure we'd looked at every poem Creech mentions. We even wrote some "inspired by" poems of our own. It was a lot of fun and it helped A LOT when we read the book. I think the kids enjoyed the book a lot more. <br/><br/>Will continue read</span>
  <span id="freeText11116621899641018939" style="display:none"><b>Review #3 - January 10, 2018</b><br /><br />I love poetry and I love this book. I read it to my students every year and I never get tired of it. (It also makes me cry every freaking time.) <br /><br />In my class, we look at one or two famous poems each week. This year, before I read the book, I made sure we'd looked at every poem Creech mentions. We even wrote some "inspired by" poems of our own. It was a lot of fun and it helped A LOT when we read the book. I think the kids enjoyed the book a lot more. <br /><br />Will continue reading to my students. Great book of poetry and a sweet story to boot! <br /><br /><br /><b>Review #2 - December 31, 2015</b><br /><br />Read it out loud to my fifth graders. They didn't seem to love it as much as the fourth graders did. Doesn't matter, I still loved it. Fantastic book!<br /><br /><b>Review #1 - January 10, 2014</b><br />I love this book. I've read it several times and the last time I read it was to the fourth graders. They loved it as well. It's a very tender story about a boy and his dog, all told through poetry. Creech does an excellent job weaving famous poetry throughout the story. It was a great introduction to our poetry unit and only took 2 days to read. (You can read the whole book in about 30 minutes.)<br /><br />Makes me cry. Every. Time.</span>

I thought it was a weird way for us to get into writing poetry. However, as an adult I read it to one of my students and really enjoyed it. I don't think my student understood the book or liked it as much as I did, but I found this to be a great read.<br/><br/>The main character, Jack, hates poetry, but when his teacher, Ms. Stretchberry, won't stop giving her class poetry assignments Jack had to do them. When </span>
  <span id="freeText1192641877967078772" style="display:none">When I young my reading teacher read this book to the class and I did not appreciate the story. I thought it was a weird way for us to get into writing poetry. However, as an adult I read it to one of my students and really enjoyed it. I don't think my student understood the book or liked it as much as I did, but I found this to be a great read.<br /><br />The main character, Jack, hates poetry, but when his teacher, Ms. Stretchberry, won't stop giving her class poetry assignments Jack had to do them. When Jack started writing poetry, he discovered that he was actually very good and had a lot to say. This book is his story throughout that process. </span>

<div id="review_2609783154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2609783154" />
    <a title="Nolan Mitchell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/87369303-nolan-mitchell"><img alt="Nolan Mitchell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543424457p2/87369303.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2609783154?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nolan Mitchell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nolan Mitchell" href="/user/show/87369303-nolan-mitchell">Nolan Mitchell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2609783154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18080978188921859995">I thought Love that Dog was a poetry book that I could tolerate. Not because it was short and quick and high on lexile, but because it wasn't like other poems where it a bunch of words that rhyme and don't really make any sense. It is about a boy named Jack who doesn't really think he is good at poetry and isn't for boys like him. As the book goes on he figures out that he is actually ok at poetry just like his idol Mr. Walter Dean Myers. Jack sees that he can write about his everyday things and</span>
  <span id="freeText18080978188921859995" style="display:none">I thought Love that Dog was a poetry book that I could tolerate. Not because it was short and quick and high on lexile, but because it wasn't like other poems where it a bunch of words that rhyme and don't really make any sense. It is about a boy named Jack who doesn't really think he is good at poetry and isn't for boys like him. As the book goes on he figures out that he is actually ok at poetry just like his idol Mr. Walter Dean Myers. Jack sees that he can write about his everyday things and turn it into poetry. Like when he writes about he dog named Sky and is in Love with him, but everything comes to an end. Overall Love that Dog is a good book to start off your career on reading poetry. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18080978188921859995" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('pBTQVNx5zJcWvn2uq2VCzeKR4/M3T111uOIpnwUGJf70LMckvpqGxNXhnmJgdYuXTtKQDlyUz5yfa+5cmWmabA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/11633.Sharon_Creech">About Sharon Creech</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/11633.Sharon_Creech"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1595481856p3/11633.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/11633.Sharon_Creech">Sharon Creech
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,370
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14602837913351406019">	I was born in South Euclid, Ohio, a suburb of Cleveland, and grew up there with my noisy and rowdy family: my parents (Ann and Arvel), my sister (Sandy), and my three brothers (Dennis, Doug and Tom).<br/>For a fictional view of what it was like growing up in my family, see Absolutely Normal Chaos. (In that book, the brothers even have the same names as my own brothers.) Our house was not only full of </span>
  <span id="freeText14602837913351406019" style="display:none">	I was born in South Euclid, Ohio, a suburb of Cleveland, and grew up there with my noisy and rowdy family: my parents (Ann and Arvel), my sister (Sandy), and my three brothers (Dennis, Doug and Tom).<br />For a fictional view of what it was like growing up in my family, see Absolutely Normal Chaos. (In that book, the brothers even have the same names as my own brothers.) Our house was not only full of us Creeches, but also full of friends and visiting relatives.<br />In the summer, we usually took a trip, all of us piled in a car and heading out to Wisconsin or Michigan or, once, to Idaho. We must have been a very noisy bunch, and I'm not sure how our parents put up with being cooped up with us in the car for those trips. The five-day trip out to Idaho when I was twelve had a powerful effect on me: what a huge and amazing country! I had no idea then that thirty-some years later, I would recreate that trip in a book called Walk Two Moons.<br />One other place we often visited was Quincy, Kentucky, where my cousins lived (and still live) on a beautiful farm, with hills and trees and swimming hole and barn and hayloft. We were outside running in those hills all day long, and at night we'd gather on the porch where more stories would be told. I loved Quincy so much that it has found its way into many of my books--transformed into Bybanks, Kentucky. Bybanks appears in Walk Two Moons and Chasing Redbird and Bloomability. Bybanks also makes a brief appearance (by reference, but not by name) in The Wanderer.<br />When I was young, I wanted to be many things when I grew up: a painter, an ice skater, a singer, a teacher, and a reporter. It soon became apparent that I had little drawing talent, very limited tolerance for falling on ice, and absolutely no ability to stay on key while singing. I also soon learned that I would make a terrible reporter because when I didn't like the facts, I changed them. It was in college, when I took literature and writing courses, that I became intrigued by story-telling. Later, I was a teacher (high school English and writing) in England and in Switzerland. While teaching great literature, I learned so much about writing: about what makes a story interesting and about techniques of plot and characterization and point of view. I started out writing novels for adults: The Recital and Nickel Malley were both written and published while I was living in England (these books were published in England only and are now out of print.) But the next book was Absolutely Normal Chaos, and ever since that book I have written mainly about young people. Walk Two Moons was the first of my books to be published in America. When it received the Newbery Medal, no one was more surprised than I was. I'm still a little bit in shock.<br />After Walk Two Moons came Chasing Redbird, Pleasing the Ghost, Bloomability, The Wanderer, and Fishing in the Air. I hope to be writing stories for a long, long time.<br />I am married to Lyle Rigg, who is the headmaster of The Pennington School in Pennington, New Jersey, and have two grown children, Rob and Karin. Being with my family is what I enjoy most. The next-best thing is writing stories.<br /><br />© Sharon Creech</span>
  <a data-text-id="14602837913351406019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
